With the first sunbeam the watch come and open the church , and not only be the colonel there , but the king in person , come to see what have happen to the sentinel .
He find them both sit hand in hand on the step in front of the altar , and immediately know his daughter again , and take her in his arm , thank God and her deliverer .
He make no objection to what they have arrange , and so Christian the smith hold his wedding with the princess , and get half the kingdom at once , and the whole of it when the king die .
As for the other sentry , with so many door and window open , no doubt they have run away , and go into the Prussian service .
And a for what Christian say he saw , he have be drink more wine than be good for him .
The Three Brothers Translated from the German of the Brothers Grimm .
There be once a man who have three son , and no other possession beyond the house in which he live .
Now the father love his three son equally , so that he could not make up his mind which of them should have the house after his death , because he do not wish to favour any one more than the others .
And he do not want to sell the house , because it have belong to his family for generation ; otherwise he could have divide the money equally amongst them .
At last an idea strike him , and he say to his son : ` You must all go out into the world , and look about you , and each learn a trade , and then , when you return , whoever can produce the best masterpiece shall have the house . '
The son be quite satisfied .
The eldest wish to be a blacksmith , the second a barber , and the third a fencing-master .
They appoint a time when they be to return home , and then they all set out .
It so happen that each find a good master , where he learn all that be necessary for his trade in the best possible way .
The blacksmith have to shoe the king 's horse , and think to himself , ` Without doubt the house will be yours ! '
The barber shave the best men in the kingdom , and he , too , make sure that the house would be his .
The fencing-master receive many a blow , but he set his teeth , and would not allow himself to be trouble by them , for he think to himself , ` If you be afraid of a blow you will never get the house . '
When the appoint time have come the three brother meet once more , and they sit down and discuss the best opportunity of show off their skill .
Just then a hare come run across the field towards them .
` Look ! '
say the barber , ` here come something in the nick of time ! '
seize basin and soap , make a lather whilst the hare be approach , and then , a it run at full tilt , shave it moustache , without cut it or injure a single hair on it body . '
I like that very much indeed , ' say the father .
` Unless the others exert themselves to the utmost , the house will be yours . '
Soon after they saw a man drive a carriage furiously towards them .
` Now , father , you shall see what I can do ! '
say the blacksmith , and he sprang after the carriage , tear off the four shoe of the horse a it be go at the top of it speed , and shoe it with four new one without check it pace .
` You be a clever fellow ! '
say the father , ` and know your trade as well a your brother .
I really do n't know to which of you I shall give the house . '
Then the third son say , ` Father , let me also show you something ; ' and , a it be begin to rain , he draw his sword and swung it in cross cut above his head , so that not a drop fell on him , and the rain fell heavy and heavy , till at last it be come down like a waterspout , but he swing his sword faster and faster , and keep as dry a if he be under cover .
When the father saw this he be astonish , and say , ` You have produce the great masterpiece : the house be yours . '
Both the other brother be quite satisfied , and praise him too , and a they be so fond of each other they all three remain at home and ply their trade : and a they be so experienced and skilful they earn a great deal of money .
So they live happily together till they be quite old , and when one be take ill and die the two others be so deeply grieve that they be also take ill and die too .
And so , because they have all be so clever , and so fond of each other , they be all lay in one grave .
The Snow-queen Translated from the German of Hans Andersen by Miss Alma Alleyne .
There be once a dreadfully wicked hobgoblin .
One day he be in capital spirit because he have make a looking-glass which reflect everything that be good and beautiful in such a way that it dwindle almost to nothing , but anything that be bad and ugly stand out very clearly and look much bad .
The most beautiful landscape look like boil spinach , and the best people look repulsive or seem to stand on their head with no body ; their face be so change that they could not be recognise , and if anyone have a freckle you might be sure it would be spread over the nose and mouth .
That be the best part of it , say the hobgoblin .
But one day the looking-glass be drop , and it break into a million-billion and more piece .
And now come the great misfortune of all , for each of the piece be hardly as large a a grain of sand and they fly about all over the world , and if anyone have a bit in his eye there it stay , and then he would see everything awry , or else could only see the bad side of a case .
For every tiny splinter of the glass possess the same power that the whole glass have .
Some people get a splinter in their heart , and that be dreadful , for then it begin to turn into a lump of ice .
The hobgoblin laugh till his side ache , but still the tiny bit of glass fly about .
And now we will hear all about it .
In a large town , where there be so many people and house that there be not room enough for everybody to have garden , live two poor child .
They be not brother and sister , but they love each other just as much a if they be .
Their parent live opposite one another in two attic , and out on the lead they have put two box fill with flower .
There be sweet pea in it , and two rise tree , which grow beautifully , and in summer the two child be allow to take their little chair and sit out under the rose .
Then they have splendid game .
In the winter they could not do this , but then they put hot penny against the frozen window-panes , and make round hole to look at each other through .
His name be Kay , and hers be Gerda .
Outside it be snow fast .
` Those be the white bee swarm , ' say the old grandmother .
` Have they also a queen bee ? '
ask the little boy , for he know that the real bee have one .
` To be sure , ' say the grandmother .
` She fly wherever they swarm the thickest .
She be large than any of them , and never stay upon the earth , but fly again up into the black cloud .
Often at midnight she fly through the street , and peep in at all the window , and then they freeze in such pretty pattern and look like flower . '
` Yes , we have see that , ' say both child ; they know that it be true .
` Can the Snow-queen come in here ? '
ask the little girl .
` Just let her ! '
cry the boy , ' I would put her on the stove , and melt her ! '
But the grandmother stroke his hair , and tell some more story .
In the evening , when little Kay be go to bed , he jump on the chair by the window , and look through the little hole .
A few snow-flakes be fall outside , and one of the , the large , lay on the edge of one of the window-boxes .
The snow-flake grow large and large till it take the form of a maiden , dress in fine white gauze .
She be so beautiful and dainty , but all of ice , hard bright ice .
Still she be alive ; her eye glitter like two clear star , but there be no rest or peace in them .
She nod at the window , and beckon with her hand .
The little boy be frighten , and spring down from the chair .
It seem a if a great white bird have fly past the window .
The next day there be a hard frost than before .
Then come the spring , then the summer , when the rose grow and smelt more beautifully than ever .
Kay and Gerda be look at one of their picture-books -- the clock in the great church-tower have just strike five , when Kay exclaim , ` Oh !
something have sting my heart , and I 've get something in my eye ! '
The little girl throw her arm round his neck ; he wink hard with both his eye ; no , she could see nothing in them . '
I think it be go now , ' say he ; but it have not go .
It be one of the tiny splinter of the glass of the magic mirror which we have hear about , that turn everything great and good reflect in it small and ugly .
And poor Kay have also a splinter in his heart , and it begin to change into a lump of ice .
It do not hurt him at all , but the splinter be there all the same .
` Why be you cry ? '
he ask ; ` it make you look so ugly !
There 's nothing the matter with me .
Just look !
that rise be all slug-eaten , and this one be stunt !
What ugly roses they be ! '
And he begin to pull them to piece .
` Kay , what be you do ? '
cry the little girl .
And when he saw how frightened she be , he pull off another rise , and run in at his window away from dear little Gerda .
When she come later on with the picture book , he say that it be only fit for baby , and when his grandmother tell them story , he be always interrupt with , ` But -- ' and then he would get behind her and put on her spectacle , and speak just a she do .
This he do very well , and everybody laugh .
Very soon he could imitate the way all the people in the street walk and talk .
His game be now quite different .
On a winter 's day he would take a burning glass and hold it out on his blue coat and let the snow-flakes fall on it .
` Look in the glass , Gerda !
Just see how regular they be !
They be much more interesting than real flower .
Each be perfect ; they be all make accord to rule .
If only they do not melt ! '
One morning Kay come out with his warm glove on , and his little sledge hung over his shoulder .
He shout to Gerda , ' I be go to the market-place to play with the other boy , ' and away he go .
In the market-place the bold boy use often to fasten their sledge to the cart of the farmer , and then they get a good ride .
When they be in the middle of their game there drive into the square a large sledge , all white , and in it sit a figure dress in a rough white fur pelisse with a white fur cap on .
The sledge drive twice round the square , and Kay fasten his little sledge behind it and drive off .
It go quicker and quicker into the next street .
The driver turn round , and nod to Kay ina friendly way a if they have know each other before .
Every time that Kay try to unfasten his sledge the driver nod again , and Kay sit still once more .
Then they drive out of the town , and the snow begin to fall so thickly that the little boy could not see his hand before him , and on and on they go .
He quickly unfasten the cord to get loose from the big sledge , but it be of no use ; his little sledge hung on fast , and it go on like the wind .
Then he cry out , but nobody hear him .
He be dreadfully frighten .
The snowflake grow large and large till they look like great white bird .
All at once they fly aside , the large sledge stand still , and the figure who be drive stand up .
The fur cloak and cap be all of snow .
It be a lady , tall and slim , and glittering .
It be the Snow-queen .
` We have come at a good rate , ' she say ; ` but you be almost frozen .
Creep in under my cloak . '
And she set him close to her in the sledge and draw the cloak over him .
He felt a though he be sink into a snow-drift .
` Are you cold now ? '
she ask , and kiss his forehead .
The kiss be cold a ice and reach down to his heart , which be already half a lump of ice .
` My sledge !
Do n't forget my sledge ! '
He think of that first , and it be fasten to one of the great white bird who fly behind with the sledge on it back .
The Snow-queen kiss Kay again , and then he forget all about little Gerda , his grandmother , and everybody at home .
` Now I must not kiss you any more , ' she say , ` or else I should kiss you to death . '
Then away they fly over forest and lake , over sea and land .
Round them whistle the cold wind , the wolf howl , and the snow hiss ; over them fly the black shrieking crow .
But high up the moon shone large and bright , and thus Kay pass the long winter night .
In the day he sleep at the Snow-queen 's foot .
But what happen to little Gerda when Kay do not come back ?
What have become of him ?
Nobody know .
The other boy tell how they have see him fasten his sledge on to a large one which have drive out of the town gate .
Gerda cry a great deal .
The winter be long and dark to her .
Then the spring come with warm sunshine . '
I will go and look for Kay , ' say Gerda .
So she go down to the river and get into a little boat that be there .
Presently the stream begin to carry it away .
` Perhaps the river will take me to Kay , ' think Gerda .
She glide down , past tree and field , till she come to a large cherry garden , in which stand a little house with strange red and blue window and a straw roof .
Before the door stand two wooden soldier , who be shoulder arm .
Gerda call to them , but they naturally do not answer .
The river carry the boat on to the land .
Gerda call out still louder , and there come out of the house a very old woman .
She lean upon a crutch , and she wear a large sun-hat which be paint with the most beautiful flower .
` You poor little girl ! '
say the old woman .
And then she step into the water , bring the boat in close with her crutch , and lift little Gerda out .
` And now come and tell me who you be , and how you come here , ' she say .
Then Gerda tell her everything , and ask her if she have see Kay .
But she say he have not pass that way yet , but he would soon come .
She tell Gerda not to be sad , and that she should stay with her and take of the cherry tree and flower , which be good than any picture-bok , a they could each tell a story .
She then take Gerda 's hand and lead her into the little house and shut the door .
The window be very high , and the pane be red , blue , and yellow , so that the light come through in curious colour .
On the table be the most delicious cherry , and the old woman let Gerda eat as many a she like , while she comb her hair with a gold comb a she eat .
The beautiful sunny hair ripple and shone round the dear little face , which be so soft and sweet . '
I have always long to have a dear little girl just like you , and you shall see how happy we will be together . '
And a she comb Gerda 's hair , Gerda think less and less about Kay , for the old woman be a witch , but not a wicked witch , for she only enchant now and then to amuse herself , and she do want to keep little Gerda very much .
So she go into the garden and wave her stick over all the rise bush and blossom and all ; they sink down into the black earth , and no one could see where they have be .
The old woman be afraid that if Gerda saw the rose she would begin to think about her own , and then would remember Kay and run away .
Then she lead Gerda out into the garden .
How glorious it be , and what lovely scent fill the air !
All the flower you can think of blossom there all the year round .
Gerda jump for joy and play there till the sun set behind the tall cherry tree , and then she sleep in a beautiful bed with red silk pillow fill with violet , and she sleep soundly and dream a a queen do on her wedding day .
The next day she play again with the flower in the warm sunshine , and so many day pass by .
Gerda know every flower , but although there be so many , it seem to her a if one be not there , though she could not remember which .
She be look one day at the old woman 's sun-hat which have hte paint flower on it , and there she saw a rise .
The witch have forget to make that vanish when she have make the other rose disappear under the earth .
it be so difficult to think of everything .
` Why , there be no rose here ! '
cry Gerda , , and she hunt amongst all the flower , but not one be to be find .
Then she sit down and cried , but her tear fell just on the spot where a rise bush have sink , and when her warm tear water the earth , the bush come up in full bloom just a it have be before .
Gerda kiss the rose and thought of the lovely rose at home , and with them come the thought of little Kay .
` Oh , what have I be do ! '
say the little girl . '
I want to look for Kay . '
She run to the end of the garden .
The gate be shut , but she push against the rusty lock so that it come open .
She run out with her little bare foot .
No one come after her .
At last she could not run any long , and she sit down on a large stone .
When she look round she saw that the summer be over ; it be late autumn .
It have not change in the beautiful garden , where be sunshine and flower all the year round .
` Oh , dear , how late I have make myself ! '
say Gerda .
` It 's autumn already !
I can not rest ! '
And she spring up to run on .
Oh , how tired and sore her little foot grow , and it become colder and colder .
She have to rest again , and there on the snow in front of her be a large crow .
It have be look at her for some time , and it nod it head and say , ` Caw !
caw !
good day . '
Then it ask the little girl why she be alone in the world .
She tell the crow her story , and ask if he have see Kay .
The crow nod very thoughtfully and say , ` It might be !
It might be ! '
` What !
Do you think you have ? '
cry the little girl , and she almost squeeze the crow to death a she kiss him .
` Gently , gently ! '
say the crow . '
I think -- I know I think -- it might be little Kay , but now he have forget you for the princess ! '
` Does he live with a princess ? '
ask Gerda .
` Yes , listen , ' say the crow .
Then he tell her all he know .
` In the kingdom in which we be now sit live a princess who be dreadfully clever .
She have read all the newspaper in the world and have forget them again .
She be as clever a that .
The other day she come to the throne , and that be not so pleasant a people think .
Then she begin to say , `` Why should I not marry ? ''
But she want a husband who could answer when he be speak to , not one who would stand up stiffly and look respectable -- that would be too dull .
` When she tell all the Court lady , they be delight .
You can believe every word I say , ' say the crow , ' I have a tame sweetheart in the palace , and she tell me everything . '
Of course his sweetheart be a crow .
` The newspaper come out next morning with a border of heart round it , and the princess 's monogram on it , and inside you could read that every good-looking young man might come into the palace and speak to the princess , and whoever should speak loud enough to be hear would be well feed and look after , and the one who speak best should become the princess 's husband .
Indeed , ' say the crow , ` you can quite believe me .
It be as true a that I be sit here .
` Young men come in stream , and there be such a crowding and a mixing together !
But nothing come of it on the first nor on the second day .
They could all speak quite well when they be in the street , but as soon a they come inside the palace door , and saw the guard in silver , and upstairs the footman in gold , and the great hall all light up , then their wit leave them !
And when they stand in front of the throne where the princess be sit , then they could not think of anything to say except to repeat the last word she have speak , and she do not much care to hear that again .
It seem a if they be walk in their sleep until they come out into the street again , when they could speak once more .
There be a row stretch from the gate of the town up to the castle .
` They be hungry and thirsty , but in the palace they do not even get a glass of water . '
A few of the cleverest have bring some slice of bread and butter with them , but they do not share them with their neighbour , for they think , `` If he look hungry , the princess will not take him ! '' '
` But what about Kay ? '
ask Gerda .
` When do he come ?
Was he in the crowd ? '
` Wait a bit ; we be come to him !
On the third day a little figure come without horse or carriage and walk jauntily up to the palace .
His eye shone a yours do ; he have lovely curl hair , but quite poor clothes . '
` That be Kay ! '
cry Gerda with delight .
` Oh , then I have find him ! '
and she clap her hand .
` He have a little bundle on his back , ' say the crow .
` No , it must have be his skate , for he go away with his skate ! '
` Very likely , ' say the crow , ' I do not see for certain .
But I know this from my sweetheart , that when he come to the palace door and saw the royal guard in silver , and on the stair the footman in gold , he be not the least bit put out .
He nod to them , say , `` It must be rather dull stand on the stair ; I would rather go inside ! ''
` The hall blaze with light ; councillor and ambassador be walk about in noiseless shoe carry gold dish .
It be enough to make one nervous !
His boot creak dreadfully loud , but he be not frighten . '
` That must be Kay ! '
say Gerda . '
I know he have new boot on ; I have hear them creak in his grandmother 's room ! '
` They do creak , certainly ! '
say the crow .
` And , not one bit afraid , up he go to the princess , who be sit on a large pearl as round a a spinning wheel .
All the ladies-in-waiting be stand round , each with their attendant , and the lords-in-waiting with their attendant .
The nearer they stand to the door the prouder they be . '
` It must have be dreadful ! '
say little Gerda .
` And Kay do win the princess ? ' '
I hear from my tame sweetheart that he be merry and quick-witted ; he have not come to woo , he say , but to listen to the princess 's wisdom .
And the end of it be that they fell in love with each other . '
` Oh , yes ; that be Kay ! '
say Gerda .
` He be so clever ; he could do sum with fraction .
Oh , do lead me to the palace ! '
` That 's easily say ! '
answer the crow , ` but how be we to manage that ?

She may be able to advise u , for I must tell you that a little girl like you could never get permission to enter it . '

She take it out of the kitchen ; there 's plenty there , and you must be hungry .

She remember so well his clever eye , his curly hair .

In the middle of it , hang on a thick rod of gold , be two bed , shape like lily , one all white , in which lay the princess , and the other red , in which Gerda hop to find Kay .
She push aside the curtain , and saw a brown neck .
Oh , it be Kay !

He wake up , turn his head and -- it be not Kay !
It be only his neck that be like Kay 's , but he be young and handsome .

Then Gerda cry , and tell her story and all that the crow have do .

say the prince and princess , and they praise the crow , and say that they be not angry with them , but that they must not do it again .

` Would you like to fly away free ? '

And both crow bow and ask for a permanent appointment , for they think of their old age .
And they put Gerda to bed , and she fold her hand , think , a she fell asleep , ` How good people and animal be to me ! '
The next day she be dress from head to foot in silk and satin .

call the prince and princess ; and little Gerda cry , and the crow cry .

gold ! '

Creep into them ! '

She read it over three time till she know it by heart , and then put the fish in the saucepan , for she never waste anything .

I know .

The Fir-tree Translated from the German of Hans Christian Andersen .
There be once a pretty little fir-tree in a wood .
It be in a capital position , for it could get sun , and there be enough air , and all around grow many tall companion , both pine and fir .
It do not heed the warm sun and the fresh air , or notice the little peasant child who run about chatter when they come out to gather wild strawberry and raspberry .
Often they find a whole basketful and strung strawberry on a straw ; they would sit down by the little fir-tree and say , ` What a pretty little one this be ! '
The tree do not like that at all .
By the next year it have grow a whole ring taller , and the year after that another ring more , for you can always tell a fir-tree 's age from it ring .
` Oh !
if I be only a great tree like the others ! '
sigh the little fir-tree , ` then I could stretch out my branch far and wide and look out into the great world !
The bird would build their nest in my branch , and when the wind blew I would bow to it politely just like the others ! '
It take no pleasure in the sunshine , nor in the bird , nor in the rose-coloured cloud that sail over it at dawn and at sunset .
Then the winter come , and the snow lay white and sparkle all around , and a hare would come and spring right over the little fir-tree , which annoy it very much .
But when two more winter have pass the fir-tree be so tall that the hare have to run round it .
` Ah !
to grow and grow , and become great and old !
that be the only pleasure in life , ' think the tree .
In the autumn the woodcutter use to come and hew some of the tall tree ; this happen every year , and the young fir-tree would shiver a the magnificent tree fell crash and crackle to the ground , their branch hew off , and the great trunk leave bare , so that they be almost unrecognisable .
But then they be lay on waggon and drag out of the wood by horse .
` Where be they go ?
What will happen to them ? '
In spring , when the swallow and stork come , the fir-tree ask them , ` Do you know where they be take ?
Have you meet them ? '
The swallow know nothing of them , but the stork nod his head thoughtfully , say , ' I think I know .
I meet many new ship a I fly from Egypt ; there be splendid mast on the ship .
I 'll wager those be they !
They have the scent of fir-trees .
Ah !
those be grand , grand ! '
` Oh !
if I be only big enough to sail away over the sea too !
What sort of thing be the sea ?
what do it look like ? '
` Oh !
it would take much too long to tell you all that , ' say the stork , and off he go .
` Rejoice in your youth , ' say the sunbeam , ` rejoice in the sweet growing time , in the young life within you . '
And the wind kiss it and the dew wept tear over it , but the fir-tree do not understand .
Towards Christmas-time quite little tree be cut down , some not as big a the young fir-tree , or just the same age , and now it have no peace or rest for long to be away .
These little tree , which be choose for their beauty , keep all their branch ; they be put in cart and draw out of the wood by horse .
` Whither be those go ? '
ask the fir-tree ; ` they be no big than I , and one there be much small even !
Why do they keep their branch ?
Where be they take to ? '
` We know !
we know ! '
twitter the sparrow .
` Down there in the city we have peep in at the window , we know where they go !
They attain to the great splendour and magnificence you can imagine !
We have look in at the window and see them plant in the middle of the warm room and adorn with the most beautiful things-golden apple , sweet-meats , toy and hundred of candle . '
` And then ? '
ask the fir-tree , tremble in every limb with eagerness , ` and then ?
what happen then ? '
` Oh , we have n't see anything more than that .
That be simply matchless ! '
` Am I too destine to the same brilliant career ? '
wonder the fir-tree excitedly .
` That be even good than sailing over the sea !
I be sick with longing .
If it be only Christmas !
Now I be tall and grown-up like those which be take away last year .
Ah , if I be only in the cart !
If I be only in the warm room with all the splendour and magnificence !
And then ?
Then come something well , something still more beautiful , else why should they dress u up ?
There must be something great , something grander to come -- but what ?
Oh !
I be pin away !
I really do n't know what 's the matter with me ! '
` Rejoice in u , ' say the air and sunshine , ` rejoice in your fresh youth in the free air ! '
But it take no notice , and just grow and grow ; there it stand fresh and green in winter and summer , and all who saw it say , ` What a beautiful tree ! '
And at Christmas-time it be the first to be cut down .
The axe go deep into the pith ; the tree fell to the ground with a groan ; it felt bruise and faint .
It could not think of happiness , it be sad at leave it home , the spot where it have spring up ; it know , too , that it would never see again it dear old companion , or the little shrub and flower , perhaps not even the bird .
Altogether the parting be not pleasant .
When the tree come to itself again it be pack in a yard with other tree , and a man be say , ` This be a splendid one , we shall only want this . '
Then come two footman in livery and carry the fir-tree to a large and beautiful room .
There be picture hang on the wall , and near the Dutch stove stand great Chinese vas with lion on their lid ; there be armchair , silk-covered sofa , big table laden with picture-books and toy , worth hundred of pounds-at least , so the child say .
The fir-tree be place in a great tub fill with sand , but no one could see that it be a tub , for it be all hung with greenery and stand on a gay carpet .
How the tree tremble !
What be come now ?
On it branch they hang little net cut out of coloured paper , each full of sugarplum ; gild apple and nut hang down as if they be grow , over a hundred red , blue , and white taper be fasten among the branch .
Dolls as life-like a human being -- the fir-tree have never see any before be suspend among the green , and right up at the top be fix a gold tinsel star ; it be gorgeous , quite unusually gorgeous !
` To-night , ' they all say , ` to-night it will be light ! '
` Ah ! '
think the tree , ` if it be only even !
Then the taper would soon be light .
What will happen then ?
I wonder whether the tree will come from the wood to see me , or if the sparrow will fly against the window pane ?
Am I to stand here deck out thus through winter and summer ? '
It be not a bad guess , but the fir-tree have real bark-ache from sheer longing , and bark-ache in tree be just as bad a head-ache in human being .
Now the taper be light .
What a glitter !
What splendour !
The tree quiver in all it branch so much , that one of the candle catch the green , and sing it .
` Take care ! '
cry the young lady , and they extinguish it .
Now the tree do not even dare to quiver .
It be really terrible !
It be so afraid of lose any of it ornament , and it be quite bewilder by all the radiance .
And then the folding door be open , and a crowd of child rush in , a though they want to knock down the whole tree , whilst the old people follow soberly .
The child stand quite silent , but only for a moment , and then they shout again , and dance round the tree , and snatch off one present after another .
` What be they do ? '
think the tree .
` What be go to happen ? '
And the taper burn low on the branch , and be put out one by one , and then the child be give permission to plunder the tree .
They rush at it so that all it bough creak ; if it have not be fasten by the gold star at the top to the ceiling , it would have be overthrow .
The child dance about with their splendid toy , and no one look at the tree , except the old nurse , who come and peep amongst the bough , just to see if a fig or an apple have be forget . '
A story !
a story ! '
cry the child , and drag a little stout man to the tree ; he sit down beneath it , say , ` Here we be in the greenwood , and the tree will be delight to listen !
But I be only go to tell one story .
Shall it be Henny Penny or Humpty Dumpty who fell downstairs , and yet gain great honour and marry a princess ? '
` Henny Penny ! '
cry some ; ` Humpty Dumpty ! '
cried others ; there be a perfect babel of voice !
Only the fir-tree kept silent , and think , ` Am I not to be in it ?
Am I to have nothing to do with it ? '
But it have already be in it , and play out it part .
And the man tell them about Humpty Dumpty who fell downstairs and marry a princess .
The child clap their hand and cry , ` Another !
another ! '
They want the story of Henny Penny also , but they only get Humpty Dumpty .
The fir-tree stood quite astonished and thoughtful ; the bird in the wood have never relate anything like that .
` Humpty Dumpty fell downstairs and yet marry a princess !
yes , that be the way of the world ! '
think the tree , and be sure it must be true , because such a nice man have tell the story .
` Well , who know ?
Perhaps I shall fall downstairs and marry a princess . '
And it rejoice to think that next day it would be deck out again with candle , toy , glitter ornament , and fruit .
` To-morrow I shall quiver again with excitement .
I shall enjoy to the full all my splendour .
To-morrow I shall hear Humpty Dumpty again , and perhaps Henny Penny too . '
And the tree stand silent and lose in thought all through the night .
Next morning the servant come in .
` Now the dress up will begin again , ' think the tree .
But they drag it out of the room , and up the stair to the lumber-room , and put it in a dark corner , where no ray of light could penetrate .
` What do this mean ? '
think the tree .
` What be I to do here ?
What be there for me to hear ? '
And it leant against the wall , and think and think .
And there be time enough for that , for day and night go by , and no one come ; at last when some one do come , it be only to put some great box into the corner .
Now the tree be quite cover ; it seem a if it have be quite forget .
` Now it be winter out-doors , ' think the fir-tree .
` The ground be hard and cover with snow , they ca n't plant me yet , and that be why I be stay here under cover till the spring come .
How thoughtful they be !
Only I wish it be not so terribly dark and lonely here ; not even a little hare !
It be so nice out in the wood , when the snow lay all around , and the hare leapt past me ; yes , even when he leap over me : but I do n't like it then .
It 's so dreadfully lonely up here . '
` Squeak , squeak ! '
say a little mouse , steal out , follow by a second .
They sniff at the fir-tree , and then creep between it bough .
` It 's frightfully cold , ' say the little mouse .
` How nice it be to be here !
Do n't you think so too , you old fir-tree ? '
` I 'm not at all old , ' say the tree ; ` there be many much old than I be . '
` Where do you come from ? '
ask the mouse , ` and what do you know ? '
They be extremely inquisitive .
` Do tell u about the most beautiful place in the world .
Is that where you come from ?
Have you be in the storeroom , where cheese lie on the shelf , and ham hang from the ceiling , where one dance on tallow candle , and where one go in thin and come out fat ? ' '
I know nothing about that , ' say the tree .
` But I know the wood , where the sun shine , and the bird sing . '
And then it tell them all about it young day , and the little mouse have never hear anything like that before , and they listen with all their ear , and say : ` Oh , how much you have see !
How lucky you have be ! '
` I ? '
say the fir-tree , and then it think over what it have tell them .
` Yes , on the whole those be very happy time . '
But then it go on to tell them about Christmas Eve , when it have be adorn with sweet-meats and taper .
` Oh ! '
say the little mouse , ` how lucky you have be , you old fir-tree ! '
` I 'm not at all old ' say the tree . '
I only come from the wood this winter .
I be only a little backward , perhaps , in my growth . '
` How beautifully you tell story ! '
say the little mouse .
And next evening they come with four others , who want to hear the tree 's story , and it tell still more , for it remember everything so clearly and think : ` Those be happy time !
But they may come again .
Humpty dumpty fell downstairs , and yet he marry a princess ; perhaps I shall also marry a princess ! '
And then it think of a pretty little birch-tree that grow out in the wood , and seem to the fir-tree a real princess , and a very beautiful one too .
` Who be Humpty Dumpty ? '
ask the little mouse .
And then the tree tell the whole story ; it could remember every single word , and the little mouse be ready to leap on to the topmost branch out of sheer joy !
Next night many more mouse come , and on Sunday even two rat ; but they do not care about the story , and that trouble the little mouse , for now they think less of it too .
` Is that the only story you know ? '
ask the rat .
` The only one , ' answer the tree . '
I hear that on my happy evening , but I do not realise then how happy I be . '
` That 's a very poor story .
Do n't you know one about bacon or tallow candle ?
a storeroom story ? '
` No , ' say the tree .
` Then we be much oblige to you , ' say the rat , and they go back to their friend .
At last the little mice go off also , and the tree say , sigh : ` Really it be very pleasant when the lively little mice sit round and listen whilst I tell them story .
But now that 's over too .
But now I will think of the time when I shall be bring out again , to keep up my spirit . '
But when do that happen ?
Well , it be one morning when they come to tidy up the lumber-room ; they throw it really rather roughly on the floor , but a servant drag it off at once downstairs , where there be daylight once more .
` Now life begin again ! '
think the tree .
It felt the fresh air , the first ray of the sun , and there it be out in the yard !
Everything pass so quickly ; the tree quite forget to notice itself , there be so much to look at all around .
The yard open on a garden full of flower ; the rose be so fresh and sweet , hang over a little trellis , the lime-trees be in blossom , and the swallow fly about , say : ` Quirre-virre-vil , my husband have come home ; ' but it be not the fir-tree they mean .

they be all wither and yellow ; and it be lie in a corner among weed and nettle .

One of the little one run up , and tear off the gold star .
` Look what be leave on the ugly old fir-tree ! '
he cry , and stamp on the bough so that they crack under his foot .
And the tree look at all the splendour and freshness of the flower in the garden , and then look at itself , and wish that it have be leave lie in the dark corner of the lumber-room ; it think of it fresh youth in the wood , of the merry Christmas Eve , and of the little mouse who have listen so happily to the story of Humpty Dumpty .
` Too late !
Too late ! '
think the old tree .
` If only I have enjoy myself whilst I could .
Now all be over and go . '
And a servant come and cut the tree into small piece , there be quite a bundle of them ; they flicker brightly under the great copper in the brew-house ; the tree sigh deeply , and each sigh be like a pistol-shot ; so the child who be play there run up , and sit in front of the fire , gaze at it , and cry , ` Piff !
puff !
bang ! '
But for each report , which be really a sigh , the tree be think of a summer 's day in the wood , or of a winter 's night out there , when the star be shin ; it think of Christmas Eve , and of Humpty Dumpty , which be the only story it have hear , or could tell , and then the tree have burn away .
The child play on in the garden , and the young have the golden star on his breast , which the tree have wear on the happy evening of it life ; and now that be past -- and the tree have pass away -- and the story too , all end and do with .
And that 's the way with all story !
Here our Danish author end .
This be what people call sentiment , and I hope you enjoy it !
Hans , the Mermaid 's Son Translated from the Danish .
In a village there once live a smith call Basmus , who be in a very poor way .
He be still a young man , and a strong handsome fellow to boot , but he have many little child and there be little to be earn by his trade .
He be , however , a diligent and hard-working man , and when he have no work in the smithy he be out at sea fishing , or gather wreckage on the shore .
It happen one time that he have go out to fish in good weather , all alone in a little boat , but he do not come home that day , nor the follow one , so that all believe he have perish out at sea .
On the third day , however , Basmus come to shore again and have his boat full of fish , so big and fat that no one have ever see their like .
There be nothing the matter with him , and he complain neither of hunger or thirst .
He have get into a fog , he say , and could not find land again .
What he do not tell , however , be where he have be all the time ; that only come out six year later , when people get to know that he have be catch by a mermaid out on the deep sea , and have be her guest during the three day that he be miss .
From that time forth he go out no more to fish ; nor , indeed , do he require to do so , for whenever he go down to the shore it never fail that some wreckage be wash up , and in it all kind of valuable thing .
In those day everyone take what they find and get leave to keep it , so that the smith grow more prosperous day by day .
When seven year have pass since the smith go out to sea , it happen one morning , a he stand in the smithy , mend a plough , that a handsome young lad come in to him and say , ` Good-day , father ; my mother the mermaid send her greeting , and say that she have have me for six year now , and you can keep me for a long . '
He be a strange enough boy to be six year old , for he look a if he be eighteen , and be even big and strong than lad commonly be at that age .
` Will you have a bite of bread ? '
say the smith .
` Oh , yes , ' say Hans , for that be his name .
The smith then tell his wife to cut a piece of bread for him .
She do so , and the boy swallow it at one mouthful and go out again to the smithy to his father .
` Have you get all you can eat ? '
say the smith .
` No , ' say Hans , ` that be just a little bit . '
The smith go into the house and take a whole loaf , which he cut into two slice and put butter and cheese between them , and this he give to Hans .
In a while the boy come out to the smithy again .
` Well , have you get as much a you can eat ? '
say the smith .
` No , not nearly , ' say Hans ; ' I must try to find a good place than this , for I can see that I shall never get my fill here . '
Hans wish to set off at once , as soon a his father would make a staff for him of such a kind a he want .
` It must be of iron , ' say he , ` and one that can hold out . '
The smith bring him an iron rod as thick a an ordinary staff , but Hans take it and twist it round his finger , so that would n't do .
Then the smith come drag one as thick a a waggon-pole , but Hans bend it over his knee and break it like a straw .
The smith then have to collect all the iron he have , and Hans hold it while his father forge for him a staff , which be heavy than the anvil .
When Hans have get this he say , ` Many thanks , father ; now I have get my inheritance . '
With this he set off into the country , and the smith be very pleased to be rid of that son , before he eat him out of house and home .
Hans first arrive at a large estate , and it so happen that the squire himself be stand outside the farmyard .
` Where be you go ? '
say the squire . '
I be look for a place , ' say Hans , ` where they have need of strong fellow , and can give them plenty to eat . '
` Well , ' say the squire , ' I generally have twenty-four men at this time of the year , but I have only twelve just now , so I can easily take you on . '
` Very well , ' say Hans , ' I shall easily do twelve men 's work , but then I must also have as much to eat a the twelve would . '
All this be agree to , and the squire take Hans into the kitchen , and tell the servant girl that the new man be to have as much food a the other twelve .
It be arrange that he should have a pot to himself , and he could then use the ladle to take his food with .
It be in the evening that Hans arrive there , so he do nothing more that day than eat his supper -- a big pot of buck-wheat porridge , which he clean to the bottom and be then so far satisfied that he say he could sleep on that , so he go off to bed .
He sleep both well and long , and all the rest be up and at their work while he be still sleep soundly .
The squire be also on foot , for he be curious to see how the new man would behave who be both to eat and work for twelve .
But a yet there be no Hans to be see , and the sun be already high in the heaven , so the squire himself go and call on him .
` Get up , Hans , ' he cry ; ` you be sleep too long . '
Hans wake up and rub his eye .
` Yes , that 's true , ' he say , ' I must get up and have my breakfast . '
So he rise and dress himself , and go into the kitchen , where he get his pot of porridge ; he swallow all of this , and then ask what work he be to have .
He be to thresh that day , say the squire ; the other twelve men be already busy at it .
There be twelve threshing-floors , and the twelve men be at work on six of them -- two on each .
Hans must thresh by himself all that be lie upon the other six floor .
He go out to the barn and get hold of a flail .
Then he look to see how the others do it and do the same , but at hte first stroke he smash the flail in piece .
There be several flail hang there , and Hans take the one after the other , but they all go the same way , every one flying in splinter at the first stroke .
He then look round for something else to work with , and find a pair of strong beam lie near .
Next he catch sight of a horse-hide nail up on the barn-door .
With the beam he make a flail , use the skin to tie them together .
The one beam he use a a handle , and the other to strike with , and now that be all right .
But the barn be too low , there be no room to swing the flail , and the floor be too small .
Hans , however , find a remedy for this -- he simply lift the whole roof off the barn , and set it down in the field beside .
He then empty down all the corn that he could lay his hand on and thresh away .
He go through one lot after another , and it be ll the same to him what he get hold of , so before midday he have thresh all the squire 's grain , his rye and wheat and barley and oat , all mixed through each other .
When he be finish with this , he lift the roof up on the barn again , like set a lid on a box , and go in and tell the squire that the job be do .
The squire open his eye at this announcement ; and come out to see if it be really true .
It be true , sure enough , but he be scarcely delight with the mixed grain that he get from all his crop .
However , when he saw the flail that Hans have use , and learn how he have make room for himself to swing it , he be so afraid of the strong fellow , that he dare not say anything , except that it be a good thing he have get it thresh ; but it have still to be clean .
` What do that mean ? '
ask Hans .
It be explain to him that the corn and the chaff have to be separate ; a yet both be lie in one heap , right up to the roof .
Hans begin to take up a little and sift it in his hand , but he soon saw that this would never do .
He soon think of a plan , however ; he open both barn-doors , and then lay down at one end and blew , so that all the chaff fly out and lay like a sand-bank at the other end of the barn , and the grain be as clean a it could be .
Then he report to the squire that that job also be do .
The squire say that that be well ; there be nothing more for him to do that day .
Off go Hans to the kitchen , and get as much a he could eat ; then he go and take a midday nap which last till supper-time .
Meanwhile the squire be quite miserable , and make his moan to his wife , say that she must help him to find some mean to get rid of this strong fellow , for he durst not give him his leave .
She send for the steward , and it be arrange that next day all the men should go to the forest for fire-wood , and that they should make a bargain among them , that the one who come home last with his load should be hang .
They think they could easily manage that it would be Hans who would lose his life , for the others would be early on the road , while Hans would certainly oversleep himself .
In the evening , therefore , the men sit and talk together , say that next morning they must set out early to the forest , and a they have a hard day 's work and a long journey before them , they would , for their amusement , make a compact , that whichever of them come home last with his load should lose his life on the gallows .
So Hans have no objection to make .
Long before the sun be up next morning , all the twelve men be on foot .
They take all the best horse and cart , and drive off to the forest .
Hans , however , lay and sleep on , and the squire say , ` Just let him lie . '
At last , Hans think it be time to have his breakfast , so he get up and put on his clothes .
He take plenty of time to his breakfast , and then go out to get his horse and cart ready .
The others have take everything that be any good , so that he have a difficulty in scrap together four wheel of different size and fix them to an old cart , and he could find no other horse than a pair of old hack .
He do not know where it lay , but he follow the track of the other cart , and in that way come to it all right .
On come to the gate lead into the forest , he be unfortunate enough to break it in piece , so he take a huge stone that be lie on the field , seven ell long , and seven ell broad , and set this in the gap , then he go on and join the others .
These laugh at him heartily , for they have labour as hard a they could since daybreak , and have help each other to fell tree and put them on the cart , so that all of these be now load except one .
Hans get hold of a woodman 's axe and proceed to fell a tree , but he destroy the edge and break the shaft at the first blow .
He therefore lay down the axe , put his arm round the tree , and pull it up by the root .
This he throw upon his cart , and then another and another , and thus he go on while all the others forget their work , and stand with open mouth , gaze at this strange woodcraft .
All at once they begin to hurry ; the last cart be load , and they whip up their horse , so a to be the first to arrive home .
When Hans have finish his work , he again put his old hack into the cart , but they could not move it from the spot .
He be annoy at this , and take them out again , twist a rope round the cart , and all the tree , lift the whole affair on his back , and set off home , lead the horse behind him by the rein .
When he reach the gate , he find the whole row of cart stand there , unable to get any further for the stone which lay in the gap .
` What ! '
say Hans , ` can twelve men not move that stone ? '
With that he lift it and throw it out of hte way , and go on with his burden on his back , and the horse behind him , and arrive at the farm long before any of the others .
The squire be walk about there , look and look , for he be very curious to know what have happen .
Finally , he catch sight of Hans come along in this fashion , and be so frightened that he do not know what to do , but he shut the gate and put on the bar .
When Hans reach the gate of the courtyard , he lay down the tree and hammer at it , but no one come to open it .
He then take the tree and toss them over the barn into the yard , and the cart after them , so that every wheel fly off in a different direction .
When the squire saw this , he think to himself , ` The horse will come the same way if I do n't open the door , ' so he do this .
` Good day , master , ' say Hans , and put the horse into the stable , and go into the kitchen , to get something to eat .
At length the other men come home with their load .
When they come in , Hans say to them , ` Do you remember the bargain we make last night ?
Which of you be it that 's go to be hang ? '
` Oh , ' say they , ` that be only a joke ; it do n't mean anything . '
` Oh well , it do n't matter , ` say Hans , and there be no more about it .
The squire , however , and his wife and the steward , have much to say to each other about the terrible man they have get , and all be agree that they must get rid of him in some way or other .
The steward say that he would manage this all right .
Next morning they be to clean the well , and they would use of that opportunity .
They would get him down into the well , and then have a big mill-stone ready to throw down on top of him -- that would settle him .
After that they could just fill in the well , and then escape be at any expense for his funeral .
Both the squire and his wife think this a splendid idea , and go about rejoice at the thought that now they would get rid of Hans .
But Hans be hard to kill , a we shall see .
He sleep long next morning , a he always do , and finally , a he would not waken by himself , the squire have to go and call him .
` Get up , Hans , you be sleep too long , ' he cry .
Hans wake up and rub his eye .
` That 's so , ' say he , ' I shall rise and have my breakfast . '
He get up then and dress himself , while the breakfast stand wait for him .
When he have finish the whole of this , he ask what he be to do that day .
He be tell to help the other men to clean out the well .
That be all right , and he go out and find the other men wait for him .
To these he say that they could choose whichever task they like -- either to go down into the well and fill the bucket while he pull them up , or pull them up , and he alone would go down to the bottom of the well .
They answer that they would rather stay above-ground , a there would be no room for so many of them down in the well .
Hans therefore go down alone , and begin to clean out the well , but the men have arrange how they be to act , and immediately each of them seize a stone from a heap of huge block , and throw them down above him , think to kill him with these .
Hans , however , give no more heed to this than to shout up to them , to keep the hen away from the well , for they be scrap gravel down on the top of him .
They then saw that they could not kill him with little stone , but they have still the big one left .
The whole twelve of them set to work with pole and roller and roll the big mill-stone to the brink of the well .
It be with the great difficulty that they get it thrown down there , and now they have no doubt that he have get all that he want .
But the stone happen to fall so luckily that his head go right through the hole in the middle of the mill-stone , so that it sit round his neck like a priest 's collar .
At this , Hans would stay down no longer .
He come out of the well , with the mill-stone round his neck , ad go straight to the squire and complain that the other men be try to make a fool of him .
He would not be their priest , he say ; he have too little learn for that .
Saying this , he bend down his head and shake the stone off , so that it crush one of the squire 's big toe .
The squire go limp in to his wife , and the steward be send for .
He be tell that he must devise some plan for get rid of this terrible person .
The scheme he have devise before have be of no use , and now good counsel be scarce .
` Oh , no ' say the steward , ` there be good enough way yet .
The squire can send him this evening to fish in Devilmoss Lake : he will never escape alive from there , for no one can go there by night for Old Eric . '
That be a grand idea , both the squire and his wife think , and so he limp out again to Hans , and say that he would punish his men for have try to make a fool of him .
Meanwhile , Hans could do a little job where he would be free from these rascal .
He should go out on the lake and fish there that night , and would then be free from all work on the following day .
` All right , ' say Hans ; ' I be well content with that , but I must have something with me to eat -- a baking of bread , a cask of butter , a barrel of ale , and a keg of brandy .
I ca n't do with less than that . '
The squire say that he could easily get all that , so Hans get all of these tie up together , hang them over his shoulder on his good staff , and tramp away to Devilmoss Lake .
There he get into the boat , row out upon the lake , and get everything ready to fish .
As he now lay out there in the middle of the lake , and it be pretty late in the evening , he think he would have something to eat first , before start to work .
Just a he be at his busiest with this , Old Eric rise out of the lake , catch him by the cuff of the neck , whip him out of the boat , and drag him down to the bottom .
It be a lucky thing that Hans have his walking-stick with him that day , and have just time to catch hold of it when he felt Old Eric 's claw in his neck , so when they get down to the bottom he say , ` Stop now , just wait a little ; here be solid ground . '
With that he catch Old Eric by the back of the neck with one hand , and hammer away on his back with the staff , till he beat him out as flat a a pancake .
Old Eric then begin to lament and howl , beg him just to let him go , and he would never come back to the lake again .
` No , my good fellow , ' say Hans , ` you wo n't get off until you promise to bring all the fish in the lake up to the squire 's courtyard , before to-morrow morning . '
Old Eric eagerly promise this , if Hans would only let him go ; so Hans row ashore , eat up the rest of his provision , and go home to bed .
Next morning , when the squire rise and open his front door , the fish come tumble into the porch , and the whole yard be cram full of them .
He run in again to his wife , for he could never devise anything himself , and say to her , ` What shall we do with him now ?
Old Eric have n't take him .
I be certain that all the fish be out of the lake , for the yard be just fill with them . '
` Yes , that 's a bad business , ' say she ; ` you must see if you ca n't get him send to Purgatory , to demand tribute . '
The squire therefore make his way to the men 's quarter , to speak to Hans , and it take him all his time to push his way along the wall , under the eaves , on account of the fish that fill the yard .
He thank Hans for have fish so well , and say that now he have an errand for him , which he could only give to a trusty servant , and that be to journey to Purgatory , and demand three year tribute , which , he say , be owe to him from that quarter .
` Willingly , ' say Hans ; ` but what road do I go , to get there ? '
The squire stand , and do not know what to say , and have first to go in to his wife to ask her .
` Oh , what a fool you be ! '
say she , ` ca n't you direct him straight forward , south through the wood ?
Whether he get there or not , we shall be quit of him . '
Out go the squire again to Hans .
` The way lie straight forward , south through the wood , ' say he .
Hans then must have his provision for the journey ; two baking of bread , two cask of butter , two barrel of ale , and two keg of brandy .
He tie all these up together , and get them on his shoulder hang on his good walking-stick , and off he tramp southward .
After he have get through the wood , there be more than one road , and he be in doubt which of them be the right one , so he sit down and open up his bundle of provision .
He find he have leave his knife at home , but by good chance , there be a plough lying close at hand , so he take the coulter of this to cut the bread with .
As he sit there and take his bite , a man come rid past him .
` Where be you from ? '
say Hans .
` From Purgatory , ' say the man .
` Then stop and wait a little , ' say Hans ; but the man be in a hurry , and would not stop , so Hans run after him and catch the horse by the tail .
This bring it down on it hind leg , and the man go fly over it head into a ditch .
` Just wait a little , ' say Hans ; ' I be go the same way . '
He get his provision tie up again , and lay them on the horse 's back ; then he take hold of the rein and say to the man , ` We two can go along together on foot . '
As they go on their way Hans tell the stranger both about the errand he have on hand and the fun he have have with Old Eric .
The other say but little but he be well acquaint with the way , and it be no long time before they arrive at the gate .
There both horse and rider disappear , and Hans be leave alone outside .
` They will come and let me in presently , ' he think to himself ; but no one come .
He hammer at the gate ; still no one appear .
Then he get tired of wait , and smash at the gate with his staff until he knock it in piece and get inside .
A whole troop of little demon come down upon him and ask what he want .
His master 's compliment , say Hans , and he want three year ' tribute .
At this they howl at him , and be about to lay hold of him and drag him off ; but when they have get some rap from his walking-stick they let go again , howl still loud than before , and run in to Old Eric , who be still in bed , after his adventure in the lake .
They tell him that a messenger have come from the squire at Devilmoss to demand three year ' tribute .
He have knock the gate to piece and bruise their arm and leg with his iron staff .
` Give him three year ' !
give him ten ! '
shout Old Eric , ` only do n't let him come near me . '
So all the little demon come drag so much silver and gold that it be something awful .
Hans fill his bundle with gold and silver coin , put it on his neck , and tramp back to his master , who be scar beyond all measure at see him again .
But Hans be also tire of service now .
Of all the gold and silver he bring with him he let the squire keep one half , and he be glad enough , both for the money and at get rid of Hans .
The other half he take home to his father the smith in Furreby .
To him also he say , ` Farewell ; ' he be now tire of living on shore among mortal men , and prefer to go home again to his mother .
Since that time no one have ever see Hans , the Mermaid 's son .
Peter Bull From the Danish .
There once live in Denmark a peasant and his wife who own a very good farm , but have no child .
They often lament to each other that they have no one of their own to inherit all the wealth that they possess .
They continue to prosper , and become rich people , but there be no heir to it all .
One year it happen that they own a pretty little bull-calf , which they call Peter .
It be the pretty little creature they have ever see -- so beautiful and so wise that it understand everything that be say to it , and so gentle and so full of play that both the man and his wife come to be a fond of it a if it have be their own child .
One day the man say to his wife , ' I wonder , now , whether our parish clerk could teach Peter to talk ; in that case we could not do good than adopt him a our son , and let him inherit all that we possess . '
` Well , I do n't know , ' say his wife , ` our clerk be tremendously learn , and know much more than his Paternoster , and I could almost believe that he might be able to teach Peter to talk , for Peter have a wonderfully good head too .
You might at least ask him about it . '
Off go the man to the clerk , and ask him whether he think he could teach a bull-calf that they have to speak , for they wish so much to have it a their heir .
The clerk be no fool ; he look round about to see that no one could overhear them , and say , ` Oh , yes , I can easily do that , but you must not speak to anyone about it .
It must be do in all secrecy , and the priest must not know of it , otherwise I shall get into trouble , a it be forbidden .
It will also cost you something , a some very expensive book be require . '
That do not matter at all , the man say ; they would not care so very much what it cost .
The clerk could have a hundred dollar to begin with to buy the book .
He also promise to tell no one about it , and to bring the calf round in the evening .
He give the clerk the hundred dollar on the spot , and in the evening take the calf round to him , and the clerk promise to do his best with it .
In a week 's time he come back to the clerk to hear about the calf and see how it be thrive .
The clerk , however , say that he could not get a sight of it , for then Peter would long after him and forget all that he have already learn .
He be get on well with his learning , but another hundred dollar be need , a they must have more book .
The peasant have the money with him , so he give it to the clerk , and go home again with high hope .
In another week the man come again to learn what progress Peter have make now .
` He be get on very well , ' say the clerk . '
I suppose he ca n't say anything yet ? '
say the man .
` Oh , yes , ' say the clerk , ` he can say `` Moo '' now . '
` Do you think he will get on with his learn ? '
ask the peasant .
` Oh , yes , ' say the clerk , ` but I shall want another hundred dollar for book .
Peter ca n't learn well out of the one that he have get . '
` Well , well , ' say the man , ` what must be spend shall be spend . '
So he give the clerk the third hundred dollar for book , and a cask of good old ale for Peter .
The clerk drink the ale himself , and give the calf milk , which he think would be good for it .
Some week pass , during which the peasant do not come round to ask after the calf , be frighten lest it should cost him another hundred dollar , for he have begin to squirm a bit at have to part with so much money .
Meanwhile the clerk decide that the calf be as fat a it could be , so he kill it .
After he have get all the beef out of the way he go inside , put on his black clothes , and make his way to the peasant 's house .
As soon a he have say ` Good-day ' he ask , ` Has Peter come home here ? '
` No , indeed , he have n't , ' say the man ; ` surely he have n't run away ? ' '
I hope , ' say the clerk , ` that he would not behave so contemptibly after all the trouble I have have to teach him , and all that I have spend upon him .
I have have to spend at least a hundred dollar of my own money to buy book for him before I get him so far on .
He could say anything he like now , so he say to-day that he long to see his parent again .
I be willing to give him that pleasure , but I be afraid that he would n't be able to find the way here by himself , so I make myself ready to go with him .
When we have get outside the house I remember that I have leave my stick inside , and go in again to get it .
When I come out again Peter have go off on his own account .
I think he would be here , and if he be n't I do n't know where he be . '
The peasant and his wife begin to lament bitterly that Peter have run away in this fashion just when they be to have so much joy of him , and after they have spend so much on his education .
The bad of it be that now they have no heir after all .
The clerk comfort them a best he could ; he also be greatly distressed that Peter should have behave in such a way just when he should have gain honour from his pupil .
Perhaps he have only go astray , and he would advertise him at church next Sunday , and find out where anyone have see him .
Then he bid them ` Good-bye , ' and go home nad din on a good fat veal roast .
Now it so happen that the clerk take in a newspaper , and one day he chance to read in it column of a new merchant who have settle in a town at some distance , and whose name be ` Peter Bull . '
He put the newspaper in his pocket , and go round to the sorrow couple who have lose their heir .
He read the paragraph to them , and add , ' I wonder , now , whether that could be your bull-calf Peter ? '
` Yes , of course it be , ' say the man ; ` who else would it be ? '
His wife then speak up and say , ` You must set out , good man , and see about him , for it be him , I be perfectly certain .
Take a good sum of money with you , too ; for who know but what he may want some cash now that he have turn a merchant ! '
Next day the man get a bag of money on his back and a sandwich in his pocket , and his pipe in his mouth , and set out for the town where the new merchant live .
It be no short way , and he travel for many day before he finally arrive there .
He reach it one morning , just at daybreak , find out the right place , and ask if the merchant be at home .
Yes , he be , say the people , but he be not up yet .
` That do n't matter , ' say the peasant , ` for I be his father .
Just show me up to his bedroom . '
He be show up to the room , and as soon a he enter it , ad catch sight of the merchant , he recognise him at once .
He have the same broad forehead , the same thick neck , and same red hair , but in other respect he be now like a human be .
The peasant rush straight up to him and take a firm hold of him . '
O Peter , ' say he , ` what a sorrow you have cause u , both myself and your mother , by run off like this just a we have get you well educate !
Get up , now , so that I can see you properly , and have a talk with you . '
The merchant think that it be a lunatic who have make his way in to him , and think it best to take thing quietly .
` All right , ' say he , ' I shall do so at once . '
He get out of bed and make haste to dress himself .
` Ay , ' say the peasant , ` now I can see how clever our clerk be .
He have do well by you , for now you look just like a human be .
If one do n't know it , one would never think that it be you we get from the red cow ; will you come home with me now ? '
` No , ' say the merchant , ' I ca n't find time just now .
I have a big business to look after . '
` You could have the farm at once , you know , ' say the peasant , ` and we old people would retire .
But if you would rather stay in business , of course you may do so .
Are you in want of anything ? '
` Oh , yes , ' say the merchant ; ' I want nothing so much a money .
A merchant have always a use for that . ' '
I can well believe that , ' say the peasant , ` for you have nothing at all to start with .
I have bring some with me for that very end . '
With that he empty his bag of money out upon the table , so that it be all cover with bright dollar .
When the merchant saw what kind of man he have before him he begin to speak him fair , and invite him to stay with him for some day , so that they might have some more talk together .
` Very well , ' say the peasant , ` but you must call me `` Father . '' ' '
I have neither father nor mother alive , ' say Peter Bull . '
I know that , ' say the man ; ` your real father be sell at Hamburg last Michaelmas , and your real mother die while calve in spring ; but my wife and I have adopt you a our own , and you be our only heir , so you must call me `` Father . '' '
Peter Bull be quite willing to do so , and it be settle that he should keep the money , while the peasant make his will and leave to him all that he have , before he go home to his wife , and tell her the whole story .
She be delighted to hear that it be true enough about Peter Bull -- that he be no other than their own bull-calf .
` You must go at once and tell the clerk , ' say she , ` and pay him the hundred dollar of his own money that he spend upon our son .
He have earn them well , and more besides , for all the joy he have give u in have such a son and heir . '
The man agree with this , and thank the clerk for all he have do , and give him two hundred dollar .
Then he sell the farm , and remove with his wife to the town where their dear son and heir be live .
To him they give all their wealth , and live with him till their dying day .
The Bird ` Grip ' Translated from the Swedish .
It happen once that a king , who have a great kingdom and three son , become blind , and no human skill or art could restore to him his sight .
At last there come to the palace an old woman , who tell him that in the whole world there be only one thing that could give him back his sight , and that be to get the bird Grip ; his song would open the King 's eye .
When the king 's eldest son heard this he offer to bring the bird Grip , which be keep in a cage by a king in another country , and carefully guard a his great treasure .
The blind king be greatly rejoice at his son 's resolve , fit him out in the best way he could , and let him go .
When the prince have ride some distance he come to an inn , in which there be many guest , all of whom be merry , and drank and sang and play at dice .
This joyous life please the prince so well that he stay in the inn , take part in the playing and drinking , and forget both his blind father and the bird Grip .
Meanwhile the king wait with both hope and anxiety for his son 's return , but a time go on and nothing be hear of him , the second prince ask leave to go in search of his brother , as well a to bring the bird Grip .
The king grant his request , and fit him out in the fine fashion .
But when the prince come to the inn and find his brother among his merry companion , he also remain there and forget both the bird Grip and his blind father .
When the king notice that neither of his son return , although a long time have pass since the second one set out , he be greatly distressed , for not only have he lose all hope of get back his sight , but he have also lose his two eldest son .
The young now come to him , and offer to go in search of his brother and to bring the bird Grip ; he be quite certain that he would succeed in this .
The king be unwilling to risk his third son on such an errand , but he beg so long that his father have at last to consent .
This prince also be fit out in the fine manner , like his brother , and so ride away .
He also turn into the same inn a his brother , and when these saw him they assail him with many entreaty to remain with them and share their merry life .
But he answer that now , when he have find them , his next task be to get the bird Grip , for which his blind father be long , and so he have not a single hour to spare with them in the inn .
He then say farewell to his brother , and rode on to find another inn in which to pass the night .
When he have ride a long way , and it begin to grow dark , he come to a house which lay deep in the forest .
Here he be receive in a very friendly manner by the host , who put his horse into the stable , and lead the prince himself into the guest-chamber , where he order a maid-servant to lay the cloth and set down the supper .
It be now dark , and while the girl be lay the cloth and set down the dish , and the prince have begin to appease his hunger , he hear the most piteous shriek and cry from the next room .
He spring up from the table and ask the girl what those cry be , and whether he have fall into a den of robber .
The girl answer that these shriek be hear every night , but it be no living be who utter them ; it be a dead man , who life the host have take because he could not pay for the meal he have have in the inn .
The host far refuse to bury the dead man , a he have leave nothing to pay the expense of the funeral , and every night he go and scourge the dead body of his victim .
When she have say this she lift the cover off one of the dish , and the prince saw that there lay on it a knife and an axe .
He understand then that the host meant to ask him by this what kind of death he prefer to die , unless he be willing to ransom his life with his money .
He then summon the host , give him a large sum for his own life , and pay the dead man 's debt as well , besides pay him for bury the body , which the murderer now promise to attend to .
The prince , however , felt that his life be not safe in this murderer 's den , and ask the maid to help him to escape that night .
She reply that the attempt to do so might cost her her own life , a the key of the stable in which the prince 's horse stand lay under the host 's pillow ; but , a she herself be a prisoner there , she would help him to escape if he would take her along with him .
He promise to do so , and they succeed in get away from the inn , and rode on until they come to another far away from it , where the prince get a good place for the girl before proceed on his journey .
As he now ride all alone through a forest there meet him a fox , who greet him in a friendly fashion , and ask him where he be go , and on what errand he be bent .
The prince answer that his errand be too important to be confide to everyone that he meet .
` You be right in that , ' say the fox , ` for it relate to the bird Grip , which you want to take and bring home to your blind father ; I could help you in this , but in that case you must follow my counsel . '
The prince think that this be a good offer , especially a the fox be ready to go with him and show him the way to the castle , where the bird Grip sit in his cage , and so he promise to obey the fox 's instruction .
When they have traverse the forest together they saw the castle at some distance .
Then the fox give the prince three grain of gold , one of which he be to throw into the guard-room , another into the room where the bird Grip sat , and the third into it cage .
He could then take the bird , but he must beware of stroke it ; otherwise it would go ill with him .
The prince take the grain of gold , and promise to follow the fox 's direction faithfully .
When he come to the guard-room of the castle he throw one of the grain in there , and the guard at once fell asleep .
The same thing happen with those who keep watch in the room beside the bird Grip , and when he throw the third grain into it cage the bird also fell asleep .
When the prince get the beautiful bird into his hand he could not resist the temptation to stroke it , whereupon it awake and begin to scream .
At this the whole castle wake up , and the prince be take prisoner .
As he now sit in his prison , and bitterly lament that his own disobedience have bring himself into trouble , and deprive his father of the chance of recover his sight , the fox suddenly stand in front of him .
The prince be very pleased to see it again , and receive with great meekness all it reproach , as well a promise to be more obedient in the future , if the fox would only help him out of his fix .
The fox say that he have come to assist him , but he could do no more than advise the prince , when he be bring up for trial , to answer ` yes ' to all the judge 's question , and everything would go well .
The prince faithfully follow his instruction , so that when the judge ask him whether he have mean to steal the bird Grip he say ` Yes , ' and when the judge ask him if he be a master-thief he again answer ` Yes . '
When the king heard that he admit be a master-thief , he say that he would forgive him the attempt to steal the bird if he would go to the next kingdom and carry off the world 's most beautiful princess , and bring her to him .
To this also the prince say ` Yes . '
When he leave the castle he meet the fox , who go along with him to the next kingdom , and when they come near the castle there , give him three grain of gold -- one to throw into the guard-room , another into the princess 's chamber , and the third into her bed .
At the same time he strictly warn him not to kiss the princess .
The prince go into the castle , and do with the grain of gold a the fox have tell him , so that sleep fell upon everyone there ; but when he have take the princess into his arm he forget the fox 's warning , at the sight of her beauty , and kiss her .
Then both she and all the others in the castle woke ; the prince be take prisoner , and put into a strong dungeon .
Here the fox again come to him and reproach him with his disobedience , but promise to help him out of this trouble also if he would answer ` yes ' to everything they ask him at his trial .
The prince willingly agree to this , and admit to the judge that he have mean to steal the princess , and that he be a master-thief .
When the king learn this he say he would forgive his offence if he would go to the next kingdom and steal the horse with the four golden shoe .
